Tafarrud (Individual Narration): Definition and Significance in Hanafi Usul
Defines tafarrud linguistically and technically, with two definitions and its role as a diagnostic tool.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tafarrud linguistically and technically, distinguish between the definitions of al-Miyanashi and al-Malibari, and explain why tafarrud is not a defect but a potential indicator of hidden flaws.

Core Idea
Tafarrud is not a defect; it is a tool that alerts the critic to possible hidden flaws in a hadith.

The term 'tafarrud' appears frequently in hadith criticism and usul al-fiqh discussions. Understanding its precise meaning and scope is essential for evaluating the strength of ahad reports. This lesson unpacks the linguistic roots, compares two technical definitions, and clarifies the role of tafarrud as a diagnostic instrument rather than an automatic cause of weakness.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic root of tafarrud
Identify the root f-r-d and understand its core meaning of oneness and solitude. All derivations from this root revolve around this concept, as confirmed by Ibn Faris.
The term 'fard' also means 'odd' or 'solitary'.
2
Examine al-Miyanashi's technical definition
Al-Miyanashi defined tafarrud as a trustworthy narrator's sole transmission from a shaykh, without other narrators from that source. Note the limitation: only trustworthy narrators are considered.
The passage suggests this definition is too narrow because it excludes weak narrators.
3
Contrast with al-Malibari's broader definition
Al-Malibari's definition encompasses any solitary narration regardless of the narrator's reliability. This aligns with the actual usage of hadith critics, who apply 'tafarrud' to both strong and weak narrators.
Critics say 'tafarrada bihi al-Zuhri' (trustworthy) and 'tafarrada bihi Ibn Abi Uways' (weak) alike.
4
Understand tafarrud as a diagnostic tool
Tafarrud is not inherently a defect (illa) but a revealer (kashif) that may indicate hidden flaws. It prompts further investigation, not automatic rejection. Ibn Rajab notes that early scholars considered solitary narrations as 'not followed' (la yutaba'), meaning they require support.
In Hanafi usul, a solitary hadith (fard/gharib) is ahad, which is zanni and must meet additional conditions for acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
أثر التَّفَرَّد في اختلاف الحديث، وأثر ذلك في اختلاف الفقهاء
التَّفَرُّدُ في اللغة:
مأخوذ من الفعل الثلاثي المزيد بحرفين (تَفَرَّدَ). يقال: فَرَدَ بالأمر والرأي انْفَرَدَ وفَرَدَ الرجلُ : كَانَ وحده مُنْفَرِداً لا ثاني مَعَهُ.
وَفَرَّدَ برأيه : اسْتَبَدَّ.
وَقَدْ أشار ابن فارس إلى أن جميع تراكيب واشتقاقات هَذَا الأصل تدل عَلَى الوحدة . إِذْ قَالَ: «الفاء والراء والدال أصل صَحِيح يدل عَلَى وحدة. من ذَلِكَ: الفرد وَهُوَ الوتر، والفارد والفرد: الثور المنفرد ...».
التفرد في الاصطلاح
عرفه أبو حفص الميانشي الفرد بأنه: ما انفرد بروايته بعض الثقات عن شيخه دون سائر الرُّوَاة عن ذَلِكَ الشيخ. ويظهر من هَذَا التعريف بعض القصور في دخول بعض أفراد المُعَرَّف في حقيقة التعريف، إِذْ قَصَرَه عَلَى انفراد الثقة فَقَطْ عن شيخه.
وعرّف الدكتور حمزة المليباري التفرد وبيّن كيفية حصوله، فَقَالَ: «يراد بالتفرد: أن يروي شخص من الرُّوَاة حديثاً دون أن يشاركه الآخرون».
وهذا التعريف الأخير أعم من التعريف الأول، فإنه شامل لتفرد الثقة وغيره، وعليه تدل تصرفات نقاد الْمُحَدِّثِينَ وجهابذة الناقلين، ولقد كثر في تعبيراتهم: حَدِيث غريب، أو تفرّد بهِ فُلان، أو هَذَا حَدِيث لا يعرف إلا من هَذَا الوجه، أو لا نعلمه يروى عن فُلان إلا من حَدِيث فُلان، ونحوها من التعبيرات.
ولربما كَانَ الحامل للميانشي عَلَى تخصيص التعريف بالثقات دون غيرهم، أن رِوَايَة الضعيف لا اعتداد بِهَا عِنْدَ عدم المتابع والعاضد ولكن من الناحية التنظيرية نجد الْمُحَدِّثِيْنَ عِنْدَ تشخيصهم لحالة التفرد لا يفرقون بَيْنَ كون المتفرد ثقة أو ضعيفاً، فيقولون مثلاً: تفرد به الزهري كَمَا يقولون: تفرد به ابن أبي أويس. وبهذا المعنى يظهر الترابط الواضح بَيْنَ المعنى اللغوي والمعنى الاصطلاحي، إِذْ إنهما يدوران في حلقة التفرد عما يماثله. والتفرد ليس بعلة في كُلّ أحواله، ولكنه كاشف عن العلة مرشد إلى وجودها، وفي هَذَا يقول الحافظ ابن رجب الحنبلي: (وأما أكثر الحفاظ المتقدمين فإنهم يقولون في الْحَدِيْث إذا تفرد بِهِ واحد - وإن لَم يرو الثقات خلافه -: إنه لا يتابع
The second topic: The effect of tafarrud (individual narration) in hadith difference, and its effect in the difference of jurists. Tafarrud linguistically: derived from the triliteral verb with two extra letters (tafarrada). It is said: farada bil-amri wa al-ra'i anfarada, and farada al-rajulu: he was alone, solitary without a second. And farrada bira'yihi: he was independent. Ibn Faris indicated that all structures and derivations of this root indicate oneness. He said: 'Fa, Ra, Dal is a sound root indicating oneness. From that: al-fard meaning the odd one, and al-farid/al-fard: the solitary bull...' Tafarrud in terminology: Abu Hafs al-Miyanashi defined al-fard as: what some trustworthy narrators narrated alone from their shaykh without other narrators from that shaykh. Some deficiency appears in this definition regarding the inclusion of some individuals of the defined in the reality of the definition, as he restricted it to the individuality of the trustworthy only from his shaykh. Dr. Hamzah al-Malibari defined tafarrud and explained how it occurs, saying: 'By tafarrud is meant: that a person among the narrators narrates a hadith without others participating with him.' This latter definition is more general than the first, as it includes the tafarrud of the trustworthy and others, and upon it are the actions of hadith critics and expert transmitters. Their expressions frequently include: a gharib hadith, or so-and-so was unique in narrating it, or this hadith is only known from this chain, or we do not know it narrated from so-and-so except through the hadith of so-and-so, and similar expressions. Perhaps what motivated al-Miyanashi to specify the definition to trustworthy narrators to the exclusion of others is that the narration of a weak narrator is not considered when there is no follow-up or support. But from a theoretical perspective, we find hadith scholars when diagnosing the state of tafarrud do not differentiate between whether the solitary narrator is trustworthy or weak. They say, for example: al-Zuhri was unique in narrating it, just as they say: Ibn Abi Uways was unique in narrating it. In this sense, the clear connection between the linguistic and technical meanings appears, as they both revolve around the circle of uniqueness from what is similar. Tafarrud is not a defect in all its states, but it is a revealer of the defect and a guide to its existence. In this regard, al-Hafiz Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali says: 'Most of the early hadith masters, when a hadith is narrated alone by one person—even if trustworthy narrators do not narrate contrary to it—say: it is not followed.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the term 'tafarrud' (individual narration) in hadith studies. It begins with the linguistic origins, tracing it to the root f-r-d meaning oneness and solitude. Then it presents the technical definition given by Abu Hafs al-Miyanashi, who restricts tafarrud to narrations by trustworthy narrators alone from a particular shaykh. The author notes a limitation: this excludes narrations by weak narrators, even though such narrations also exhibit tafarrud. A broader definition by Dr. Hamzah al-Malibari is offered, encompassing any solitary narration regardless of the narrator's reliability, which aligns with the practice of hadith critics. The passage then explains why al-Miyanashi might have limited his definition (weak narrators' reports are disregarded without corroboration) but argues that in theory, critics apply the term to both strong and weak narrators. Finally, it clarifies that tafarrud is not inherently a defect but a tool that may reveal hidden flaws, quoting Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali that early scholars considered a solitary narration as not being followed (i.e., requiring support) even if not contradicted.

Key Terms
التفرد (al-tafarrud): A narrator's sole transmission of a hadith without others sharing it from that source.
الفرد (al-fard): A hadith narrated by only one narrator at some level of the chain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of tafarrud is more comprehensive and aligns with the practice of hadith critics?
A) Al-Miyanashi's definition (trustworthy only).
B) Al-Malibari's definition (any narrator).
C) Ibn Rajab's definition.
D) The linguistic definition.

Key Takeaway
Tafarrud is a diagnostic tool, not a defect; it alerts the critic to potential hidden flaws without automatically weakening the hadith.

Parsing a Sanad: Narrator Identification and Its Fiqh Impact
Enumerating narrators with a critical note on identification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the narrators in a hadith chain and explain how variations in the sanad contribute to juristic differences in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Each narrator in a sanad carries biographical weight — a missing or disputed name can shift a ruling between schools.

This passage presents a list of narrators from a hadith chain, likely related to the issue of wiping over socks (al-mash 'ala al-khuffayn). In Hanafi usul, the reliability and identity of each narrator determines whether a hadith is accepted as proof. The note about Hamzah ibn al-Mughirah shows how careful identification resolves ambiguity and strengthens the chain for juristic derivation.

Guided Path
1
List the Narrators
Write down all the names as they appear in the passage, noting any superscript numbers that refer to footnotes.
The list includes al-Aswad ibn Hilal, Abu Umamah al-Bahili, Bishr ibn Qahif, etc.
2
Identify the Ambiguity
Read the note at the end: some versions have 'Ibn al-Mughirah from his father' without naming the son. Recognize that this is a gap in identification.
3
Apply the Clarification
Imam al-Nawawi identified the unnamed narrator as Hamzah ibn al-Mughirah. Confirm how this affects the chain's continuity and reliability.
Now the sanad is fully specified, removing doubt about the transmitter.
4
Link to Fiqh Dispute
Consider why this matters: the hadith of wiping over socks is used by Hanafis to support the permissibility of mash during wudu. A weak or ambiguous chain would weaken the argument.
Other schools may have different versions of the same hadith, leading to divergent rulings.

الرواة (al-ruwat): The narrators whose names are listed; each with a biographical note.

Hadith Matn Variance: 'Complete' vs 'Make Up' the Prayer
Listing narrators and variant wordings of a hadith on prayer completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify variant wordings in a hadith matn and explain how such variations can lead to differing legal rulings among schools.

Core Idea
Matn variants produce ikhtilaf: 'fātimū' obliges completing in full; 'fāqdū' allows making up what was missed.

This lesson examines a hadith about what a latecomer should do after the imam's taslim. The matn has two widely transmitted wordings: 'fātimū' (complete the prayer) and 'fāqdū' (make up what you missed). We will trace the transmission of each wording through key narrators and see how this textual variance becomes a source of legal disagreement, particularly between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith and Its Context
The hadith is about a person joining the prayer late: after the imam says taslim, what should the latecomer do? The key phrase is the Prophet's instruction.
This hadith is often cited in discussions about the latecomer's prayer (masbuq).
2
Extract the Two Variant Wordings
Find the two main wordings in the Arabic text: 'fātimū' (أَتِمُّوا) and 'fāqdū' (اقْضُوا). Note their literal meanings: 'complete' vs 'make up'.
3
Map the Narrators to Each Wording
Using the list in the passage, note which narrators transmitted 'fātimū' and which transmitted 'fāqdū'. For example, Abd al-Rahman ibn Yaqub al-Harqi transmitted 'fātimū', while Muhammad ibn Sirin transmitted 'fāqdū' exclusively.
Some narrators (e.g., Hammam ibn Munabbih) have both wordings through different chains.
4
Link Each Wording to Potential Legal Implications
Consider how each wording might affect the ruling: 'fātimū' suggests the latecomer completes his prayer as if he started from the beginning (obligation to pray the full units). 'fāqdū' suggests he makes up what he missed, implying he starts with what he caught and adds what is missing (lighter obligation?).
Hanafi and Shafi'i schools use these wordings to justify different positions on the masbuq's prayer.
5
Evaluate the Epistemological Weight of Each Wording
Assess the reliability of each transmission route. The passage shows multiple chains for each wording, some via Malik (strong) and others via ahad narrations. Hanafi usul may prefer one wording over another based on riwayah strength or practice of the people of Madinah.
This step introduces the concept of tarjih (preference) in usul.

Chapter Two / Variance in the Matn. Abd al-Rahman ibn Yaqub al-Harqi. And no variance from his son regarding it. He narrated from him with the wording 'fātimū' (complete). Recorded by Malik, and via him al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and al-Bukhari in al-Qira'a Khalf al-Imam, and al-Tahawi. And recorded via other than Malik: al-Bukhari in al-Qira'a and Muslim. Muhammad ibn Sirin. No variance from him; he narrated with wording 'fāqdū' (make up). His narration recorded by Ahmad, al-Bukhari in al-Qira'a, and Muslim in the Sahih from Abu Rafi'. No variance from him; he narrated with wording 'fāqdū'. His narration with Ahmad. Hammam ibn Munabbih. Recorded from him by Abd al-Razzaq, and via him Muslim, Abu Awana, and al-Bayhaqi with wording 'fātimū'. And Ahmad recorded from Abd al-Razzaq with wording 'fāqdū'. Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf; variance from him in wording. Among those who narrated from him with 'fātimū' on this face:... (then listing narrators from him: al-Zuhri, Uqayl, Shu'ayb, Yahya ibn Sa'id, Yazid ibn al-Had, Yunus, Ma'mar, and then Umar ibn Abi Salama and his narrator Sa'd ibn Ibrahim).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a hadith transmitter analysis. The author, in Chapter Two on variance in the matn (text), lists the narrators of a well-known hadith about what to say after the imam's 'Samiʻa Allāhu liman ḥamidah'. The hadith has two main wordings: 'fātimū' (complete the prayer) and 'fāqdū' (make up what you missed). The author meticulously documents which narrators transmitted which wording, and through which chains, citing major hadith collections. For example, ʻAbd al-Raḥmān ibn Yaʻqūb al-Ḥarqī transmitted 'fātimū', while Muḥammad ibn Sīrīn and Abū Rāfiʻ transmitted 'fāqdū'. The author also shows that sometimes the same narrator (e.g., Hammām ibn Munabbih) has both versions through different routes. This illustrates how textual variations in hadith can lead to different legal rulings among fuqahā’.

Key Terms
مَتْن (matn): The text of a hadith, as opposed to its chain of narrators (sanad).
فَأَتِمُّوا (fātimū): 'Complete (the prayer)' – one of two variant wordings in this hadith.
فَاقْضُوا (fāqdū): 'Make up (the prayer)' – the other variant wording.

Textual Variation and Tarjīḥ bi al-Kathrah in the Hadith of Missed Prayers
Analyzing narrator word choice in a single hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the impact of narrating by meaning on hadith texts and apply the principle of preferring the more frequently transmitted wording to resolve textual discrepancies.

Core Idea
Narrating by meaning creates textual variants; frequency of transmission resolves which wording is authoritative.

In this lesson, we analyze a well-known hadith regarding missed prayers. The hadith is transmitted with two different wordings: 'اقضوا' (make up) and 'أتموا' (complete). By examining the chain of narrators, we see that the variation arose because narrators sometimes conveyed the meaning rather than the exact wording. This case illustrates how Hanafi usul employs the principle of tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah (preference by frequency) to determine the most reliable wording, which in turn affects the fiqh ruling derived from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its variations
Recognize that the hadith about missed prayers is narrated with two different key verbs. Look at the list of narrators to see who transmitted which wording.
The two wordings are 'اقضوا' (make up) and 'أتموا' (complete).
2
Trace the source of variation
Note that both wordings trace back to a single narrator, Ibn Abi Dhi'b, who heard it from al-Zuhri. His own students transmitted it differently, indicating that they conveyed the meaning rather than the exact words.
This is a classic case of al-riwāyah bi al-maʿnā.
3
Understand the principle of tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah
Learn that when textual variants exist, the preferred version is the one transmitted by the larger number of reliable narrators. This principle is used by al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar.
Tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah is a method of resolving contradictory narrations.
4
Apply the principle to this case
Count the narrators for each wording to determine which is more frequently transmitted. The passage implies that one wording has more support.
From the passage: 'اقضوا' has at least 3 narrators (Hammad, Adam, Ahmad) while 'أتموا' has at least 2 (Ibn Abi Fudayk, Abu al-Nadr).
5
Connect to fiqh implications
Recognize that the chosen wording affects the ruling: 'اقضوا' implies redoing the missed prayer from the beginning, while 'أتموا' implies completing it from the point of interruption. The Hanafi school relies on the preferred wording.
The preferred wording 'اقضوا' leads to the ruling that missed prayers must be performed in full.

Commentary
This passage is a case study in textual variation resulting from narrating hadith by meaning (al-riwāyah bi al-maʿnā). The author lists the narrators who transmitted a well-known hadith about making up missed prayers, showing that some narrated it with the word 'اقضوا' (make up) while others narrated it with 'أتموا' (complete). The key figure is Ibn Abi Dhi'b, who heard the hadith from al-Zuhri from two different students (Abu Salamah and Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab). His own students then transmitted it with different wordings: Hammad ibn Salamah narrated it as 'اقضوا', while Ibn Abi Fudayk narrated it as 'أتموا'. The passage notes that each wording was followed by other reliable narrators, creating two parallel textual traditions. The author concludes that this divergence arose because narrators sometimes conveyed the meaning rather than the exact wording. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar is cited as resolving such discrepancies by giving preference to the more frequently transmitted version (tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah). The passage does not delve into the fiqh implications, but the difference between 'make up' and 'complete' can affect rulings on whether one must redo a missed prayer or simply complete it from the point of interruption.

Key Terms
الرواية بالمعنى (al-riwāyah bi al-maʿnā): Transmitting a hadith using the reporter's own words while preserving the intended meaning.
الترجيح بالكثرة (al-tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah): Preferring one narration over another based on the greater number of reliable transmitters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The hadith was abrogated and later replaced
D) Scribes made copying errors

Key Takeaway
Narrating hadith by meaning can lead to textual variants; the Hanafi usul principle of tarjīḥ bi al-kathrah resolves such discrepancies by preferring the more frequently transmitted wording.

Analyzing Multiplicity of Narrators in Usul al-Fiqh
This passage lists 23 narrators who transmitted a hadith from al-Zuhri, documenting the multiplicity of transmission paths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a list of narrators from a common source and explain how the multiplicity of transmission paths influences the hadith's classification (mashhur vs. ahad) and its evidentiary value in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The number of narrators from a single source helps assess the hadith's transmission frequency—mashhur—and its impact on legal rulings.

This passage appears to be a simple list of names, but in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, such lists serve a critical purpose. By enumerating the narrators who transmitted a hadith from a single teacher (here, al-Zuhri), the author documents the multiplicity of transmission paths. This multiplicity is used to classify the hadith as mashhur (widely transmitted) versus ahad (isolated), which directly affects its evidentiary weight in deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common source
All narrators listed are transmitting from one teacher, al-Zuhri. Locate this common link in the chain.
The common source is often a key figure; here it is al-Zuhri, a famous tabi'i.
2
Count the number of narrators
Count how many individuals are listed as transmitting from al-Zuhri. This number is significant for classification.
The passage lists 23 narrators. In Hanafi usul, multiple independent transmissions from one teacher can raise the hadith from ahad to mashhur under certain conditions.
3
Classify the hadith by transmission level
Apply the Hanafi classification: if there are three or more independent paths in every generation, it may be mutawatir; if less but still multiple from one teacher, it is often considered mashhur. Here, the multiplicity at one level suggests mashhur, though full tawatur would require multiplicity at all levels.
Hanafis define mashhur as a hadith transmitted by three or more Companions but not reaching tawatur. The listing of many narrators from al-Zuhri indicates widespread transmission at that level.
4
Note textual variation implication
The author's purpose in listing these narrators is to highlight that their versions of the hadith may contain textual differences (ikhtilaf al-matn). These differences can lead to juristic disagreement.
Hanafi usul scholars weigh such variations against qiyas and istihsan when deriving rulings.
5
Apply Hanafi evidentiary hierarchy
If the hadith is classified as mashhur, it takes precedence over ahad hadith and can be used to specify a general Quranic ruling (takhsis) or to support qiyas. Understand that this listing is not merely biographical; it is a tool for evaluating the hadith's authority.
In Hanafi thought, mashhur hadith holds a middle ground between mutawatir and ahad: its meaning is certain enough to be used as a proof in fiqh.

Key Terms

Tracing Chain Variants in the Tha'laba Hadith
Listing multiple transmission chains for a hadith about sadaqah on jewelry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate chain variations in a hadith and explain how such variations impact the determination of a fiqh ruling.

Core Idea
Differences in a hadith's chain can alter narrator identity, affecting authenticity and legal application.

This passage presents a meticulous inventory of the various chains through which a hadith about sadaqah on jewelry has been transmitted, focusing on the narrator Tha'laba. The author records discrepancies in the name of the companion and his father across different compilations. This textual analysis is a foundational skill in usul al-fiqh, as identifying the correct sanad determines whether a hadith is accepted as evidence and how it is interpreted. In this lesson, you will learn to systematically trace such variations and appreciate their significance for Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Hadith Subject
Understand the hadith topic: sadaqah on jewelry from a companion named Tha'laba. Note the uncertainty in the narrator's full name across chains.
2
List the Key Transmitters
Identify the common link: al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri. Then list the sub-narrators from al-Zuhri: Tha'laba ibn Saghir, Tha'laba ibn Abi Saghir, Abd Allah ibn Tha'laba, Ibn Tha'laba, etc.
The variation occurs at the level of the companion's name.
3
Map the Variations
Create a mental or written table of the different names as recorded by different compilers (e.g., Bukhari, Abu Dawud, al-Daraqutni). Note which name appears where.
This step requires careful attention to detail.
4
Analyze the Implications
Consider how the uncertainty about the narrator's identity might affect the reliability of the hadith. Does it weaken the hadith? Does it create ambiguity in the ruling derived from it?
Multiple authentic chains can strengthen, but confusion in names may lead to weakness if not resolved.
5
Relate to Hanafi Usul
In Hanafi fiqh, if a hadith has a weak chain due to such ambiguity, it may not be used as a primary dalil. Discuss how this hadith is treated in Hanafi works (e.g., whether it is used to support zakat on jewelry).
Hanafi jurists often prefer clarity in sanad; unresolved variations may lead to reliance on other evidence.

Chapter Three: Difference in the Chain and Text. It requires that Tha'laba ibn Saghir be other than Tha'laba ibn Abi Saghir, and Allah knows best. I have earnestly attempted to collect the chains of the hadith and investigate the differences contained therein, and I will detail that, so I say: The hadith was previously mentioned from the narration of al-Nu'man ibn Rashid, and from him Hammad ibn Zaid, and there is difference on this path of al-Nu'man. The hadith was narrated by others besides al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri, and the same difference occurred in the name of its narrator. Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the chain of transmission (sanad) for a hadith concerning the companion Tha'laba, likely regarding sadaqah on jewelry. The author meticulously lists the various chains by which the hadith has been transmitted, highlighting the discrepancy in the name of the narrator (Tha'laba) and his father. Some chains name him as Tha'laba ibn Saghir, others as Tha'laba ibn Abi Saghir, and yet others as 'Abd Allah ibn Tha'laba or Ibn Tha'laba. The author shows that even within the same transmitter (al-Nu'man ibn Rashid), there are variations in the name. This textual analysis is crucial for usul al-fiqh because identifying the correct narrator affects the authenticity and applicability of the hadith. The author does not resolve the discrepancy here but documents it thoroughly, which is a typical method in hadith criticism to trace how differences in sanad lead to differences in understanding the text.

Key Terms
الاختلاف في السند والمتن (al-ikhtilāf fī al-sanad wa al-matn): Differences in the chain of transmission (sanad) and the text (matn) of a hadith, which can lead to variations in fiqh rulings.
ثعلبة بن صغير (Thaʿlaba ibn Ṣaghīr): One of the variant names for the companion narrator of the hadith about sadaqah on jewelry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The reliability of a hadith as a legal proof depends on its chain; variations in the sanad must be meticulously examined before deriving a fiqh ruling.

Hadith Criticism: Mudtarib and Mu'allal in the Umm Salama Narration
Hadith criticism showing mudtarib and mu'allal defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the defects of mudtarib and mu'allal bi al-irsal in a hadith, and apply hadith criticism methods to a specific narration.

Core Idea
A hadith can be weakened by irreconcilable variations (mudtarib) and hidden chain defects (mu'allal).

The hadith of Umm Salama regarding the Fajr prayer on the day of Nahr is examined to demonstrate how textual and chain variations can undermine a narration's reliability. This lesson takes you through the process of identifying mudtarib and irsal defects in a single hadith, following the method of classical hadith critics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith and Its Claimed Ruling
Locate the main claim: the Prophet ordered Umm Salama to join him for Fajr on the day of Nahr in Mecca. This is the ruling the hadith would support.
The ruling would be about the permissibility or merit of performing Fajr on that specific day.
2
Examine the Chain Variations from Abu Mu'awiya
List the different chains transmitted from Abu Mu'awiya. Notice that all come through Hisham ibn 'Urwa, but some include Zaynab while others omit her. This is the first sign of inconsistency.
Compare the chains in the Arabic text: some have Zaynab, some do not.
3
Observe the Textual Variations (Mudtarib)
Compare the different wordings: one version says 'salat al-subh' (dawn prayer), another says 'al-duha' (forenoon), and another omits the prayer time. This indicates mudtarib in the text.
Mudtarib means conflicting versions that cannot be reconciled.
4
Identify the Irsal Defect via the Alternate Chain
Note that Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna reports the hadith directly from Hisham's father from Umm Salama, without Zaynab. This mursal version is likely the correct one, and Abu Mu'awiya's addition of Zaynab (wasl) is an error.
The hadith is therefore mu'allal bi al-irsal, defective due to being mursal.
5
Evaluate the Overall Reliability
Because of both mudtarib in text and irsal in chain, the hadith is weak. It cannot be used as evidence for a ruling.
Hanafi usul would reject this hadith; only if one version could be preferred would it be considered, but here none is preferrable.

Source Text

الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
قال: حدثنا هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أبي سلمة، عن أم سلمة: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أمرها أن توافي معه صلاة الصبح يوم النحر بمكة.
(٢) فهذا الحديث مضطرب المتن، وقد اضطرب فيه على أبي معاوية، ثم إن الحديث معل بالإرسال والصواب فيه الإرسال والوصل فيه خطأ أخطأ فيه أبو معاوية، وسأتكلم عن اضطراب متنه ثم أشرح كيف أنه معل بالإرسال. فأبو معاوية رواه عنه عدة من الرواة، وقد تغير متن الحديث عند كل راو من الرواة عن أبي معاوية فالحمل عليه إذن، وبيان ذلك:
قد روى الحديث أسد بن موسى (٣) عن أبي معاوية، عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أبي سلمة، عن أم سلمة قالت أمرها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن توافي معه صلاة الصبح بمكة (٤).
وقد روى الحديث أبو كريب (٥): محمد بن العلاء، عن أبي معاوية، عن هشام، عن أبيه عن زينب عن أم سلمة قالت: أمرها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن توافي مكة صلاة الصبح يوم النحر
ورواه عبد الله بن جعفر الرقي (٧)، عن أبي معاوية، عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أبي سلمة، عن أم سلمة: أن النبي أمرها أن توافي معه يوم النحر بمكة (٨).
ورواه أبو خيثمة: زهير بن حرب (٩)، عن أبي معاوية، قال: حدثنا هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أم سلمة، عن أم سلمة: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أمرها أن توافي صلاة الصبح يوم النحر بمكة (١٠).
ورواه محمد بن عمرو (٢) السوسي، عن أبي معاوية، عن هشام، عن أبيه، عن زينب عن أم سلمة: أن النبي أمرها أن توافي الضحى معه بمكة يوم النحر (٣).
ورواه يحيى بن يحيى النيسابوري (٤)، عن أبي معاوية، عن هشام، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أبي سلمة، عن أم سلمة.
ورواه سعيد بن سليمان، عن أبي معاوية عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن زينب بنت أم سلمة، عن أم سلمة: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أمرها أن توافيه صلاة الصبح بمكة يوم النحر (٦): أن رسول الله أمرها أن توافي صلاة الصبح يوم النحر بمكة).
هكذا اضطرب فيه أبو معاوية، واختلف الرواة عنه فيه قال ابن التركماني مضطرب سنداً ومتنا (٧).
وقال الطحاوي: «تأملنا هذا الحديث، فوجدناه إنما دار بهذا المعنى على أبي معاوية، ووجدنا أبا معاوية قد اضطرب فيه (٨). وحديث أبي معاوية معل بالإرسال - كما سبق -.
فقد رواه سفيان بن عيينة (٩)، عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن أم سلمة رضي الله عنها - أن رسول الله أمرها أن تصلي الفجر بمكة يوم النحر (١٠).
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. He said: We were told by Hisham ibn 'Urwa, from his father, from Zaynab bint Abi Salama, from Umm Salama: that the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) ordered her to join him for the dawn prayer on the day of Nahr in Mecca. (2) This hadith has a confused text, and Abu Mu'awiya was confused about it. Moreover, the hadith is defective by being mursal; the correct version is the mursal one, and the connecting (wasl) is an error that Abu Mu'awiya committed. I will discuss the confusion in its text, then explain how it is defective by irsal. Abu Mu'awiya narrated it, and several narrators narrated from him; the text of the hadith changed with each narrator from Abu Mu'awiya, so the blame lies with him. The explanation: Asad ibn Musa (3) narrated the hadith from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham ibn 'Urwa, from his father, from Zaynab bint Abi Salama, from Umm Salama, who said: The Messenger of God ordered her to join him for the dawn prayer in Mecca (4). And Abu Kurayb (5): Muhammad ibn al-'Ala' narrated the hadith from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham, from his father, from Zaynab, from Umm Salama, who said: The Messenger of God ordered her to join him in Mecca for the dawn prayer on the day of Nahr. And 'Abdullah ibn Ja'far al-Raqqi (7) narrated it from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham ibn 'Urwa, from his father, from Zaynab bint Abi Salama, from Umm Salama: that the Prophet ordered her to join him on the day of Nahr in Mecca (8). And Abu Khaythama: Zuhayr ibn Harb (9) narrated it from Abu Mu'awiya, he said: Hisham ibn 'Urwa told us, from his father, from Zaynab bint Umm Salama, from Umm Salama: that the Messenger of God ordered her to join him for the dawn prayer on the day of Nahr in Mecca (10). And Muhammad ibn 'Amr (2) al-Susi narrated it from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham, from his father, from Zaynab, from Umm Salama: that the Prophet ordered her to join him for the forenoon prayer (al-duha) with him in Mecca on the day of Nahr (3). And Yahya ibn Yahya al-Naysaburi (4) narrated it from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham, from his father, from Zaynab bint Abi Salama, from Umm Salama. And Sa'id ibn Sulayman narrated it from Abu Mu'awiya, from Hisham ibn 'Urwa, from his father, from Zaynab bint Umm Salama, from Umm Salama: that the Messenger of God ordered her to join him for the dawn prayer in Mecca on the day of Nahr (6): that the Messenger of God ordered her to join him for the dawn prayer on the day of Nahr in Mecca. Thus Abu Mu'awiya was confused about it, and the narrators differed from him regarding it. Ibn al-Turkumani said: 'It is confused in chain and text' (7). And al-Tahawi said: 'We examined this hadith and found that it revolves only around Abu Mu'awiya, and we found that Abu Mu'awiya was confused about it' (8). The hadith of Abu Mu'awiya is defective by irsal, as previously mentioned. Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna (9) narrated it from Hisham ibn 'Urwa, from his father, from Umm Salama (may God be pleased with her) - that the Messenger of God ordered her to pray Fajr in Mecca on the day of Nahr (10).
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of a specific hadith regarding Umm Salama being ordered by the Prophet to join him for the dawn prayer (Fajr) on the day of Nahr (10th of Dhul Hijjah) in Mecca. The author demonstrates how the hadith is 'mudtarib' (confused) in its text and chain, and how it is 'mu'allal bi al-irsal' (defective due to being transmitted as mursal, i.e., missing a companion link). He attributes the confusion to the narrator Abu Mu'awiya, showing multiple versions from him that differ in wording and detail. For example, one version says 'salat al-subh' (dawn prayer), another says 'al-duha' (forenoon), and still another omits the prayer time or the day. This variation indicates textual confusion. Additionally, the correct chain is mursal, as reported by Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna directly from Hisham's father from Umm Salama, without Zaynab. Thus Abu Mu'awiya's addition of Zaynab (wasl) is an error. The author quotes Ibn al-Turkumani and al-Tahawi to support this assessment. This analysis is important for usul al-fiqh because it shows how differences in chain and text can render a hadith unreliable, thereby affecting the legal rulings derived from it. In Hanafi fiqh, such hadith criticism is essential for determining the strength of evidence.

Key Terms
مضطرب (mudtarib): A hadith that is conflicted in its chain and text due to irreconcilable variations, weakening its authenticity.
معل بالإرسال (mu'allal bi al-irsal): A hadith with a hidden defect where the chain is missing a link, specifically the companion, making it mursal and thus weak.
وصل (wasl): Adding a link to a chain that is otherwise missing, in this case making it marfu' but erroneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is munqati' because Hisham is weak.
C) It is shadh because it contradicts the Qur'an.
D) It is hasan because it has multiple chains.

Key Takeaway
A hadith's reliability depends on both chain continuity and textual consistency; here, mudtarib and irsal combine to render this narration unusable for legal derivation.

Identifying al-Shafi'i's Ambiguous Narrator 'al-Thiqah'
Analyzing various identifications of al-Shafi'i's trustworthy narrator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the various narrators that al-Shafi'i referred to as 'al-thiqah' and explain how this ambiguity impacts hadith authentication in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's ambiguous 'al-thiqah' reveals that narrator identification is critical for hadith acceptance.

In usul al-fiqh, the reliability of narrators is paramount. This lesson explores how al-Shafi'i's use of the vague term 'al-thiqah' (the trustworthy one) led to multiple identifications by later scholars, affecting the weight of hadith evidence. We'll examine the list of possible narrators and understand the analytical process used to pinpoint the intended individual.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the ambiguity
Identify that al-Shafi'i often used 'al-thiqah' without naming the narrator, creating a need for identification.
This ambiguity is a textual clue that scholars must resolve.
2
List the identifications
From the passage, compile the various possible narrators for each chain (e.g., from al-Awza'i, Ibn Jurayj, etc.) as given by al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi.
The identification depends on the sub-narrator after 'al-thiqah'.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why scholars considered each identification plausible, based on who transmitted from that sub-narrator.
This involves knowledge of biographical evaluation (jarh wa ta'dil).
4
Assess the impact
Consider how the identification affects the hadith's authenticity and the derived fiqh ruling, as shown in al-Tahawi's critique of al-Shafi'i's opinion on stoning before dawn.
The connected version (mawsul) vs. disconnected (mursal) depends on the identified narrator.

أقول: لا شك في أن الشافعي إنما أخذه من أبي معاوية، فهو الذي تفرد بوصله هكذا، وقد ذكر العلماء الحمل عليه فيه.
وقد شرح الطحاوي استنباط الشافعي من حديث أبي معاوية فقال: «فاحتج الشافعي كما حكى لنا المزني عنه بهذا الحديث، وَقَالَ فيه ما قَدْ دل على أنه قَدْ أباحها أن تنفر من جمع، قبل طلوع الفجر؛ لأنه لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك منهـا مـع موافاتها مكة ضحى إلا وقد خرجت من جمع قبل طلوع الفجر لبعد ما بين مكة وجمع، وفي ذلك ما قد دل على أنها قَدْ كانت رمت الجمرة قبل طلوع الفجر. قال أبو جعفر : وهذا قول لم نعلم أحداً من أهل العلم سواه قاله)، ولا ذهب إليه، فكلهم على خلافه فيه، وعلى أنه ليس لأحد من الحاج أن يرمي جمرة العقبة في الليل قبل طلوع الفجر، فتأملنا هذا الحديث، فوجدناه إنما دار بهذا المعنى على أبي معاوية. ووجدنا أبا معاوية قد اضطرب فيه ... ثم دلل على ذلك.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. Hisham, from his father, from Zaynab bint Abi Salamah, from Umm Salamah, from the Prophet similarly. Al-Bayhaqi said: It seems that al-Shafi'i took it from Abu Mu'awiyah al-Darir, and Abu Mu'awiyah narrated it connected. And al-Shashi, Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari, Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, al-Mawardi, and al-Ruyani, and al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi considered it preponderant; because although he considered him trustworthy, if he named him, he might be someone else had criticized. Rather, his refusal to name him raises suspicion. See: al-Kifayah, etc. And al-Shafi'i usually means Yahya ibn Hassan al-Tanisi, who is trustworthy. Al-Hafiz al-'Iraqi reported from some hadith experts: If al-Shafi'i says 'we were informed by the trustworthy one from Ibn Abi Dhi'b' it is Ibn Abi Fudayk; from al-Layth ibn Sa'd it is Yahya ibn Hassan; from al-Walid ibn Kathir it is Abu Usamah; from al-Awza'i it is 'Amr ibn Abi Salamah; from Ibn Jurayj it is Muslim ibn Khalid; from Salih, freed slave of al-Taw'amah it is Ibrahim ibn Abi Yahya. etc. And it is said: He meant Ibrahim ibn Isma'il, etc. Then al-Tahawi explained al-Shafi'i's deduction: He argued by this hadith that he allowed her to leave from Muzdalifah before dawn, because she could not reach Mecca at forenoon except having left before dawn, indicating she stoned before dawn. Abu Ja'far said: This is an opinion not known from any other scholar, and all are opposed. Then he examined the hadith and found it depended on Abu Mu'awiyah, and he found Abu Mu'awiyah confused in it...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of al-Shafi'i's use of the ambiguous term 'al-thiqah' (the trustworthy one). The author first lists various possibilities for whom al-Shafi'i meant, showing that this ambiguity led to different identifications. Then al-Tahawi's explanation of how al-Shafi'i used the hadith of Umm Salamah to derive a ruling on stoning before dawn is presented. The commentary highlights that the authenticity of the hadith's connected version is disputed, and that the majority of scholars opposed al-Shafi'i's opinion. This illustrates how differences in the interpretation of chains and texts directly affect fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
الثقة (al-thiqah): The trustworthy narrator, used by al-Shafi'i ambiguously without naming the specific person.
المرسل (al-mursal): A hadith with a missing link in the chain, typically between the Successor and the Prophet.
الموصول (al-mawsul): A hadith with a complete, uninterrupted chain to the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's ambiguous use of 'al-thiqah' requires careful identification based on the sub-narrator, and this directly impacts the acceptance of hadith in usul al-fiqh.

Critique of the Ibn Mas'ud Hadith on Raf' al-Yadayn
Critique of a hadith and its application in fiqh ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the strength of the Ibn Mas'ud hadith on raising hands and explain why it is not accepted as evidence in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A weak hadith cannot override established practice; usul al-hadith criticism determines admissibility.

The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud is often cited to argue that the Prophet only raised his hands once in prayer. This lesson examines the critical evaluation of this narration by major hadith scholars. Understanding why this hadith is rejected is essential for proper istinbat in the issue of raf' al-yadayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the evidence
The hadith reports that Ibn Mas'ud prayed with the Prophet and the Prophet did not raise his hands except at the first takbir. This is the basis for the claim that raf' al-yadayn should be limited.
The hadith is quoted in the passage but not in full.
2
Examine al-Bukhari's clarification
Al-Bukhari explains that the hadith of Jabir ibn Samurah, which also mentions a prohibition of raising hands, refers specifically to the tashahhud, not the standing parts. He argues that if the hadith were general, it would also forbid raising at the opening takbir and Eid takbirs.
Al-Bukhari's comment shows contextual understanding of the narration.
3
Analyze the criticisms by Ibn al-Mubarak, Abu Hatim, and Abu Dawud
These scholars explicitly declared the Ibn Mas'ud narration weak or erroneous. Ibn al-Mubarak said 'It is not proven,' Abu Hatim called it a mistake, and Abu Dawud said it is not authentic in that wording.
These criticisms are from major hadith critics.
4
Understand the Zaydi position and its flaws
The Zaydis accept the hadith as authentic and therefore reject raising hands at the opening takbir. However, the majority of scholars reject the hadith due to its weakness.
The Zaydi position is a minority view in this issue.
5
Conclude the status of the hadith
The hadith is weak (da'if) and cannot be used as evidence. Therefore, it does not affect the established practice of raising hands at the opening takbir (as practiced by Hanafis) or at multiple points (as practiced by others).
The thubut (transmission) is zanni, and the criticism makes it unreliable.

Except that the Zaydis denied raising hands at the opening takbir.

Evaluating a Solitary Addition in Hadith: The Case of 'Bismillah'
Authenticating a hadith addition by comparing narrators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a solitary addition (ziyada) by a trustworthy narrator is rejected when a majority of equally trustworthy narrators omit it, using the hadith of water miracle as a case study.

Core Idea
A trustworthy narrator's solitary addition is rejected if all other equally trustworthy narrators omit it.

In Hanafi usul, not every addition by a trustworthy narrator is automatically accepted. When a hadith is transmitted through multiple chains and only one narrator includes an extra phrase, while many others—of equal reliability—do not, the addition is considered a mistake (wahm). This lesson examines a famous hadith about water flowing from the Prophet's fingers, where Ma'mar ibn Rashid added the word 'Bismillah'. By comparing the chains of narration from Thabit and Qatada, the author demonstrates that the addition is an error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith and the Disputed Addition
Locate the core hadith (water miracle) and the specific addition: 'Bismillah' before the command to perform wudu. Understand that this addition is claimed only by Ma'mar ibn Rashid.
2
List the Chains from Thabit and Qatada
Group the narrators who transmitted from Thabit (Sulayman ibn al-Mughira, Hammad ibn Zayd, Hammad ibn Salama) and from Qatada (Sa'id ibn Abi 'Aruba, Hammam ibn Yahya, Hisham al-Dastawa'i, Shu'ba). Note that all these narrators are reliable.
There are three from Thabit and four from Qatada, all trustworthy.
3
Compare the Reports: Who Includes the Addition?
Observe that not one of these seven narrators mentions the addition. Only Ma'mar, who is also reliable, transmitted it. This creates a conflict: one against seven.
4
Apply the Hanafi Usul Principle
Recognize that the Hanafi method prefers the majority of narrators over a solitary one, especially when the majority are equally trustworthy. The collective omission indicates that the addition is likely an error (wahm) on Ma'mar's part.
The principle: 'al-jama'ah awla min al-fard' (the group is more reliable than the individual).
5
Conclude the Rejection of the Addition
Having established that the addition is not corroborated and contradicts the majority, the conclusion is that the addition 'Bismillah' is not part of the authentic hadith. This does not impugn Ma'mar's overall trustworthiness but identifies a specific lapse.

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a hadith commonly cited for the miracle of water flowing from the Prophet's fingers. The author demonstrates a key principle in hadith criticism: when a single trusted narrator contradicts a larger number of equally trusted narrators, the solitary addition is considered a mistake (wahm). Here, Ma'mar ibn Rashid added the phrase 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) before the Prophet's command to perform wudu. The author lists numerous chains (turuq) from two main narrators, Thabit and Qatada, through multiple trustworthy students, all of whom omit this addition. The author argues that it is improbable that all these narrators would collectively forget the phrase if it were authentic. The reasoning is based on the universal method of jarh wa ta'dil and the preference for the majority (al-jama'ah) over an individual (al-fard). This does not disparage Ma'mar's trustworthiness but identifies a specific error in this particular narration.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyāda): An extra word or phrase added by a narrator to a hadith not present in other reliable versions.
تفرد (tafarrada): A narrator reporting something alone without support from other narrators.
وهم (wahm): An error or illusion in a narrator's memory or reporting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Shadhdh Increase: The Hadith of Dhul-Yadayn
Example of shadhdh increase in hadith chain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a shadhdh increase in a hadith chain and explain why it is rejected in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
An increase by a single narrator contradicting a larger reliable group is shadhdh and cannot establish a ruling.

This lesson examines a concrete example of a shadhdh (irregular) increase in the hadith of Dhul-Yadayn. The reliable narrators transmitted the hadith without the word 'takbir', but Hammad ibn Zayd added it. Because his version contradicts a larger set of trustworthy narrators, his increase is considered shadhdh and unacceptable for deriving legal rulings. The Hanafi school uses this principle to ensure that only reliable transmissions form the basis of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base hadith and its narrators
The hadith of Dhul-Yadayn (the man who mistakenly thought prayer was shortened) is narrated by multiple reliable narrators: Ayyub, Hisham, Yahya ibn Atiq, and Ibn Awn, all from Muhammad ibn Sirin from Abu Hurairah.
These narrators are considered trustworthy and their version is consistent.
2
Note the increase in one version
Hammad ibn Zayd added the word 'takbir' in his narration, stating that the Prophet said 'takbir' before the prostrations of forgetfulness.
This increase is not present in the versions of the other four narrators.
3
Compare the number and reliability of narrators
The majority (four narrators) are all reliable and agree on the absence of 'takbir'. Hammad ibn Zayd is also reliable, but he is alone in the increase.
Frequency matters: a single narrator's addition against a larger group creates suspicion.
4
Apply the rule of shadhdh
According to hadith criticism, when a reliable narrator contradicts a larger number of equally reliable narrators, his version is considered shadhdh (irregular) and rejected.
Both Hanafi and Shafi'i usul accept this principle.
5
Understand the legal consequence
Because the increase is shadhdh, it cannot be used to establish a ruling. The ruling from the hadith remains that the Prophet did not say takbir during sajdah al-sahw.
This prevents weak or isolated narrations from influencing fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال آخر للزيادة الشاذة بسبب كثرة المخالفة: روی حماد بن زید عن هشام بن حسان عن محمد بن سيرين، عن أبي هريرة حديث ذي اليدين وذكر فيه زيادة: «كبر»، فَقَالَ: «كبر ثم كبر وسجد». أخرجه أبو داود ومن طريقه البيهقي في الكبرى من طريق حماد بن زيد، عن أيوب وهشام ويحيى بن عتيق وابن عون، عن محمد، عن أبي هريرة.

It was narrated by Abu Dawood and through him al-Bayhaqi in al-Kubra via Hammad ibn Zayd, from Ayyub, Hisham, Yahya ibn Atiq, and Ibn Awn, from Muhammad, from Abu Hurairah.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage gives a concrete example of a 'shadhdh' (irregular) increase in a hadith. The hadith of Dhul-Yadayn (the man who mistakenly thought the prayer was shortened) is well-known. The reliable narrators (Ayyub, Hisham, Yahya ibn Atiq, Ibn Awn) all transmitted it without the word 'takbir' before the prostrations. But Hammad ibn Zayd's version included the extra 'takbir'. Because Hammad contradicted a larger, more reliable group, his increase is considered shadhdh and thus unacceptable for establishing a ruling.

Key Terms
الزيادة الشاذة (al-ziyadah al-shadhdhah): An addition to a hadith text or chain by a weak or contradicted narrator that is deemed irregular and unacceptable.

Key Takeaway
A solitary addition by a reliable narrator that contradicts a larger group is classified as shadhdh and rejected, ensuring that only widely transmitted elements shape Hanafi rulings.

Evaluating Hadith Ziyada Through Chain Analysis
Analyzing hadith variations and their impact on a fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of evaluating a ziyada (additional wording) by comparing multiple chains of transmission and deriving the likely ruling.

Core Idea
When many trustworthy narrators omit a ziyada, the original hadith is presumed free of it.

This lesson examines how scholars assess an additional wording (ziyada) in a hadith by analyzing its chains. The passage lists several narrators who transmitted a key hadith without the ziyada, raising questions about its authenticity. Understanding this method is crucial for weighing competing narrations in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and the ziyada
Recognize which wording is in dispute. The passage refers to a hadith narrated from Jabir, and the ziyada is some additional phrase that some narrators included but others did not.
The exact ziyada is not quoted, but its effect on the ruling is discussed.
2
List the narrators who transmitted without the ziyada
From the passage, note the six narrators who narrated via 'Amr ibn Dinar from Jabir without the ziyada: Shu'ba, Ayyub, Hammad, Sulaym, Mansur, Hisham.
They are all trustworthy; their collective omission is significant.
3
Note alternative chains also lacking the ziyada
The passage then adds that the hadith was also narrated via other routes (Abu al-Zubayr, Muharib, 'Ubaydullah) without the ziyada, strengthening the evidence.
4
Apply the usul principle
In Hanafi usul, when many reliable narrators omit a ziyada and it conflicts with a smaller number who include it, the omission is given greater weight. The absence in multiple chains weakens the ziyada's authenticity.
This is a form of tarjih (preponderance) based on quantity and reliability.
5
Connect to the resulting fiqh ruling
The absence of the ziyada supports the first madhhab, which permits difference in intention between follower and imam. The passage implies that the ziyada, if accepted, would have led to the opposite ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن

فقد روى الحديث شعبة بن الحجاج، وأيوب السختياني، وحماد بن زيد، وسليم بن حيان، ومنصور بن زاذان، وهشام الدستوائي؛ فهؤلاء جميعهم رووه عن عمرو بن دينار عن جابر، به دون ذكر الزيادة.

ثمَّ إن الحديث روي عن جابر من غير طريق عمرو بن دينار، فقد رواه أبو الزبير ومحارب بن دثار، وعبيد الله بن مقسم، ولم يذكروا هذه الزيادة مما يجعل الحكم مختلفاً عند النقاد.

أثر هذه الزيادة في اختلاف الفقهاء (اختلاف نية المأموم مع الإمام)

اختلف الفقهاء في جواز اختلاف نية المأموم مع الإمام على مذهبين:

المذهب الأول وهو جواز اختلاف نية المأموم مع الإمام، أي يجوز أن يصلي المتنفل خلف المفترض والمفترض خلف المتنفل، والمفترض خلف المفترض لفرض آخر. وعلى هذا المذهب جماهير الصحابة كما أشار إليه الماوردي - منهم عمر، وابن عمر وأبو الدرداء، وأنس -.

وذهب إلى ، من التابعين طاووس، وعطاء. وبه قال الأوزاعي، والشافعي، وسليمان بن حرب، وإسحاق بن راهويه، وهو إحدى الروايتين عن الإمام أحمد.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text.

So Shu'ba ibn al-Hajjaj, Ayyub al-Sakhtiyani, Hammad ibn Zayd, Sulaym ibn Hayyan, Mansur ibn Zadhan, and Hisham al-Dustuwa'i narrated the hadith; all of them narrated it from 'Amr ibn Dinar from Jabir, without mentioning the additional wording.

Then, the hadith was narrated from Jabir through other than 'Amr ibn Dinar: Abu al-Zubayr, Muharib ibn Dithar, and 'Ubaydullah ibn Muqsim narrated it, and they did not mention this additional wording, which makes the ruling different according to critics.

The effect of this additional wording on the difference among jurists (Difference of intention between the follower and the imam)

The jurists differed regarding the permissibility of the difference of intention between the follower and the imam into two schools:

The first school: the permissibility of difference of intention between the follower and the imam, meaning it is permissible for the supererogatory prayer to pray behind the obligatory prayer and the obligatory prayer behind the supererogatory prayer, and the obligatory prayer behind the obligatory prayer for another obligation. The majority of the Companions were upon this school, as al-Mawardi indicated - among them 'Umar, Ibn 'Umar, Abu al-Darda', and Anas.

And [the second school] was held by among the Followers: Tawus, 'Ata'; and it was said by al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Sulayman ibn Harb, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and it is one of two narrations from Imam Ahmad.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a hadith and its impact on a fiqh dispute. The author first lists multiple narrators who transmitted a hadith from 'Amr ibn Dinar from Jabir without an additional wording (ziyada). Then he notes that other narrators (Abu al-Zubayr, Muharib ibn Dithar, 'Ubaydullah ibn Muqsim) also narrated the same hadith from Jabir via different paths and also omitted this ziyada. This omission, according to critics, makes the ruling different.

Then the author examines the effect of this ziyada on a specific fiqh issue: whether it is permissible for the follower's intention (niyya) to differ from the imam's intention while praying in congregation. He presents two madhhabs:

1. The first madhhab (which is apparently supported by the hadith without the ziyada) allows difference in intention. This includes cases where a supererogatory prayer leads an obligatory prayer, or vice versa, or one obligatory prayer leads another obligatory prayer for a different obligation. This view is attributed to many Companions such as 'Umar, Ibn 'Umar, Abu al-Darda', and Anas, as al-Mawardi indicated.

2. The second madhhab (which the text begins to describe but cuts off) appears to be the opposite, held by Tawus, 'Ata', al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Sulayman ibn Harb, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and one of two narrations from Ahmad. Likely they prohibit the difference in intention.

The passage illustrates how a textual variation (presence or absence of a ziyada in a hadith) can lead to differing legal rulings among jurists, a key theme in usul al-fiqh. Note that the Hanafi position (which is not explicitly stated here) generally allows difference in intention, consistent with the first madhhab. The second madhhab (Shafi'i, etc.) typically requires congruence of intention. The author's analysis is based on the weight of narrations and their implications.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyada): An additional wording in a hadith not found in other narrations. In this passage, it is the disputed addition that some narrators included.
اختلاف نية المأموم مع الإمام (ikhtilaf niyyat al-ma'mum ma'a al-imam): The situation where the follower's intention (e.g., to pray a supererogatory prayer) differs from the imam's (e.g., to pray an obligatory prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the first madhhab (allowing difference in intention) is supported by the majority of companions, according to the passage?
A) The hadith without the ziyada was transmitted by many reliable narrators.
B) The ziyada was proven to be a fabrication.
C) The companions rejected the second madhhab outright.
D) The passage does not provide a reason.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a ziyada in multiple reliable chains can tip the balance against its authenticity, influencing the derived ruling on congregational prayer intentions.

The Dispute on Follower's Intention: Applying the Ziyada Ruling
Analyzing hadith variations and their impact on a fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two madhhabs on difference in intention between follower and imam and explain the role of the hadith ziyada in shaping the dispute.

Core Idea
The authenticity of a single ziyada can split scholars on whether a follower's intention must match the imam's.

This lesson explores the two madhhabs on permissible difference in intention between follower and imam in congregational prayer. The first madhhab, supported by many Companions and the hadith without ziyada, allows it. The second, held by Shafi'i and others, disallows it. The key lies in the evaluation of the ziyada in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether a follower's intention (e.g., to pray fard or nafl) can differ from the imam's intention in the same congregation.
This is a practical issue affecting daily prayers.
2
State the first madhhab and its evidence
The first madhhab allows any combination (nafl behind fard, fard behind nafl, one fard behind another). Their evidence includes the hadith without the ziyada and the practice of many Companions (Umar, Ibn Umar, Abu Darda, Anas).
3
State the second madhhab (from the passage)
The second madhhab (Tawus, Ata, Awza'i, Shafi'i, etc.) holds the opposite opinion. The passage cuts off but likely prohibits difference in intention, relying on the hadith that includes the ziyada.
The text is incomplete, but the context suggests this.
4
Analyze the ziyada's impact
The presence or absence of the ziyada in the hadith directly affects the ruling. If the ziyada is accepted, it imposes restriction; if rejected, the general permission stands.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi allows a person to catch a congregational prayer even if their intention differs (e.g., making up a missed prayer). Shafi'i would require the same intention, potentially causing hardship.

Source Text

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن

فقد روى الحديث شعبة بن الحجاج، وأيوب السختياني، وحماد بن زيد، وسليم بن حيان، ومنصور بن زاذان، وهشام الدستوائي؛ فهؤلاء جميعهم رووه عن عمرو بن دينار عن جابر، به دون ذكر الزيادة.

Analyzing Multiple Chains of Transmission
Listing multiple narrators and their hadith sources for a specific narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a list of multiple hadith chains (asanid) by identifying the narrators and their hadith compilers, and evaluate the relative strength of each chain based on the compilers' authority and the number of chains.

Core Idea
The strength of a hadith chain is indicated by the number and authority of the compilers who recorded it.

In usul al-fiqh, when a hadith is transmitted through multiple chains, scholars document each chain's narrators and the compilers who recorded them. This list helps assess the hadith's authenticity and potential textual variants. In this lesson, we will parse a typical list of chains and learn to draw conclusions about the reliability of each transmission route.

Guided Path
1
Identify each narrator (rawi)
Scan the list for the names of narrators. These are the individuals who transmitted the hadith from the previous generation. Note that each narrator is a companion or a tabi'i (successor).
The narrator's name is usually given first, followed by the compilers who recorded the hadith through that narrator.
2
List the hadith compilers for each narrator
For each narrator, write down the names of the compilers (mukharrijun) who recorded the hadith. For example, for Salim ibn Abd Allah ibn Umar, the compilers include al-Bukhari, Muslim, etc.
Compilers vary in authority: al-Bukhari and Muslim are considered the most reliable (sahihayn), while others like al-Tabarani are less authoritative.
3
Count the number of compilers per narrator
Count how many compilers recorded the hadith through each narrator. A larger number of compilers generally indicates wider circulation and potentially stronger authentication.
But quantity alone is not decisive; the quality of compilers matters more.
4
Assess the authority of each compiler
Evaluate the status of the compilers: al-Bukhari and Muslim (Sahihayn) are the highest, followed by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah (Sunan), then others like al-Tabarani, Ibn Abi Shayba, etc.
This assessment helps in weighing conflicting evidence when different chains have different textual variants.
5
Draw conclusions about chain strength
Based on the number and authority of compilers, rank the chains. For instance, the chain through Salim ibn Abd Allah ibn Umar is very strong (many compilers including al-Bukhari and Muslim), while the chain through Sa'd ibn Ubayda is weak (only al-Tabarani).
This process is essential in Hanafi usul to determine the strength of hadith evidence when resolving disputes.

Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a survey of the various chains of transmission for a specific hadith, though the actual text of the hadith is not provided here. The author lists different narrators (tabi'un or later) who transmitted the hadith, and for each narrator, he cites the hadith compilers who recorded it through their chain. This is a typical usul al-fiqh exercise: documenting the multiple routes of a hadith to analyze its authenticity and textual variants. Differences in chains (asānīd) can lead to differences in fiqh rulings, as the strength or wording of a hadith may vary. For example, the narrator Salim ibn Abd Allah ibn Umar has the most widespread transmission, including both al-Bukhari and Muslim, indicating a high level of acceptance. In contrast, Sa'd ibn Ubayda is only recorded by al-Tabarani, suggesting a weaker or less known chain. Such comparisons help mujtahids weigh conflicting evidence.

Key Terms
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith from the Prophet to the compiler.
متن (matn): The textual content of the hadith, which may vary with different chains.
مخرج (mukharrij): A hadith compiler who records the hadith in their collection (e.g., al-Bukhari, Muslim).

Using Chain Multiplicity in Hanafi Usul: The Example of Wiping Over Leather Socks
Listing narrators who transmitted a hadith and the scholars who recorded it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key narrators and compilers for the hadith of wiping over socks and explain how multiplicity of chains strengthens its evidentiary value in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A hadith transmitted through many chains gains strength; the list shows tawatur or istifada for the Hanafi evidence on wiping over socks.

In Hanafi usul, the strength of a hadith is not only about individual narrators but also about the number of independent chains. This passage from a work on ikhtilaf al-sanad wa al-matan lists the narrators who transmitted the hadith of wiping over leather socks and the major compilers who recorded their versions. By familiarizing yourself with these names, you gain the ability to trace the hadith's transmission and appreciate why Hanafi scholars considered it a reliable proof for a foundational ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith subject
The context suggests this list pertains to the hadith of wiping over leather socks (al-mash 'ala al-khuffayn). Confirm from the surrounding discussion or common topic.
The hadith is a key source for Hanafi fiqh on wudhu.
2
List the narrators
Scan the passage for names: Tawus, Abdullah ibn Dinar, Abdullah ibn Shaqiq, Ubaydullah ibn Abdullah, Uqbah ibn Huraith, Uqbah ibn Muslim, Atiyyah ibn Sa'd.
These are the transmitters at the level just below the Companion.
3
List the compilers
Note the names after each narrator: al-Shafi'i, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tahawi, etc. These are the scholars who recorded the hadith in their collections.
Each compiler may have recorded versions from multiple narrators.
4
Count the chains
Count the number of distinct narrators and the number of compilers. This shows multiplicity, an important factor in evaluating hadith strength.
At least 7 narrators and over 15 compilers are mentioned.
5
Assess the usul implication
Apply Hanafi usul: a hadith with multiple independent chains (mustafid) is stronger evidence than a single chain (ahad). This list demonstrates that the hadith of wiping over socks is well-attested.
Hanafis consider this hadith as hujja for the sunna status of the act.

This demonstrates the multiplicity of chains (ikhtilaf fi al-sanad) and the widespread transmission of the hadith. The list includes prominent narrators such as Tawus, Abdullah ibn Dinar, and Abdullah ibn Shaqiq, and compilers like al-Shafi'i, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and al-Bayhaqi. The footnotes provide precise references to the works where each narration is found. This type of documentation is essential for usul al-fiqh because it establishes the reliability and abundance of chains, which affects the strength of the hadith as evidence (hujjiyyah).

Key Terms
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.
متن (matn): The text or content of the hadith.

Identifying Shudhudh in Hadith Chains: Ma'mar's Narration on the Muhrim Eating Game
Analyzing a hadith chain for shudhudh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify shudhudh in a hadith chain by comparing narrators and apply the criteria of 'extreme individuality' (fardiyyah shadidah) to evaluate a narration's reliability.

Core Idea
A narration is shadh when a reliable narrator contradicts many more reliable narrators and has no corroboration from any tabaqah.

This lesson takes us into the methodology of hadith criticism as applied by Hanafi usulis. We examine a specific case where Ma'mar ibn Rashid's narration about a muhrim eating game meat is declared shadh because four other narrators from the same chains did not include his additions. Understanding shudhudh is crucial for determining which hadiths are acceptable for legal deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrators and their versions
List the five narrators of the hadith: Nafi' mawla Abi Qatadah, Ma'bad ibn Ka'b, 'Ata' ibn Yasar, Abu Salih mawla al-Taw'amah (who narrated without the two phrases), and Ma'mar ibn Rashid (who added two phrases).
The four narrators are from the same tabaqat (levels) as Ma'mar or higher.
2
Compare the transmissions
Note that the four narrators all transmitted the hadith without the two specific phrases mentioned by Ma'mar. This constitutes a contradiction (mukhalafah).
The contradiction is not in meaning but in added words.
3
Apply the shudhudh rule
Since Ma'mar's version is isolated (fardiyyah shadidah) and contradicts the more numerous and reliable narrators, his narration is deemed shadh (anomalous) and unacceptable.
Shudhudh requires both contradiction and lack of corroboration; here both conditions are met.
4
Hypothesize the cause
The author suggests Ma'mar may have confused this hadith with another he narrated about 'Uthman and a bird, leading to the insertion of the extra phrases.
This is a plausible explanation based on known narrations from Ma'mar.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of a hadith chain to demonstrate shudhudh (anomaly) in Ma'mar ibn Rashid's narration concerning a muhrim eating game meat. The author notes that four other narrators—Nafi' mawla Abi Qatadah, Ma'bad ibn Ka'b, 'Ata' ibn Yasar, and Abu Salih mawla al-Taw'amah—all transmitted the hadith without including the two specific phrases that Ma'mar added. This 'extreme individuality' (fardiyyah) coupled with contradiction (mukhalafah) confirms the shudhudh, as none of the narrators from the three tabaqat (levels) of the chain reported those phrases. The author then hypothesizes the cause: Ma'mar may have confused this hadith with another he himself narrated about 'Uthman and a bird, leading to an insertion. Footnotes provide biographical evaluations of the narrators and references to hadith collections. The lesson is purely textual criticism, establishing that Ma'mar's version is not reliable for legal ruling.

Key Terms
شذوذ (shudhudh): Anomaly in hadith where a reliable narrator contradicts more reliable narrators, causing his version to be deemed unacceptable.
فردية شديدة (fardiyyah shadidah): Extreme individuality: a narrator being alone in reporting something, which is a sign of weakness when coupled with contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Shudhudh is identified by comparing narrators; an addition by a single narrator without corroboration is rejected, even if he is trustworthy.

Detecting Idraj: The Case of 'Awsighu al-Wudu'
Analyzing a hadith with narrator insertion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an instance of idraj in a hadith and explain how distinguishing between prophetic and narrator speech affects the authority of the text in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Idraj confuses prophetic speech with narrator commentary—correct identification is essential for sound legal derivation.

In this lesson, we examine a classic example of idraj: the hadith 'Awsighu al-wudu, waylun lil-a'qabi min al-nar.' Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi and others showed that the first part is actually Abu Hurayra's own statement, not the Prophet's. Understanding such textual integrity is critical for usul al-fiqh because only the pure prophetic text carries the highest authority (sunnah). We will walk through the evidence that identifies the insertion and its implications for legal ruling derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the narrated text
Separate the hadith as transmitted by Abu Qatan and Shababa: 'Awsighu al-wudu' (Perfect the ablution) and 'Waylun lil-a'qabi min al-nar' (Woe to the heels from the Fire). Note the claim that both are from the Prophet.
This is the version containing the alleged idraj.
2
Compare with the majority transmission
List the 15 narrators who transmitted the same hadith from Shu'ba without the phrase 'Awsighu al-wudu'. Observe that they attribute only 'Waylun lil-a'qabi min al-nar' to the Prophet and treat the first phrase as Abu Hurayra's own speech.
The majority's agreement indicates the correct version.
3
Recognize the idraj
Conclude that 'Awsighu al-wudu' is inserted (mudraj) from Abu Hurayra's speech. Al-Khatib explicitly states that Abu Qatan and Shababa erred. The correct prophetic text is only the second part.
Idraj can occur at the beginning, middle, or end of a hadith.
4
Apply this distinction in usul al-fiqh
Understand that only the prophetic speech is authoritative as sunnah. The inserted phrase, being a companion's statement (mawquf), carries less weight in legal derivation. In Hanafi usul, a companion's statement is considered a source (qawl al-sahabi) but does not create obligation unless confirmed by other evidence.
This affects how a ruling derived from the hadith is categorized.
5
Derive the correct ruling
From the corrected hadith, the Prophet warns about the heels not being washed. This indicates that washing the feet is necessary (fard) in wudu, as the consequence is punishment. The phrase 'Awsighu al-wudu' is not needed; the warning alone implies the obligation.
Hanafis use this hadith as evidence for the fard of washing feet, including the heels.

Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. 417. An example of insertion at the beginning of the text is the hadith of Abu Hurayra from the Messenger of Allah: 'Perfect the ablution, woe to the heels from the Fire.' Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi narrated it in his book 'al-Fasl' via the route of Abu Qatan and Shababa - both from Shu'ba, from Muhammad ibn Ziyad, from Abu Hurayra. So his statement: 'Perfect the ablution' is inserted from the speech of Abu Hurayra. Al-Khatib and others stated this, saying: 'Abu Qatan Amr ibn al-Haytham and Shababa ibn Sawwar erred in their narration of this hadith from Shu'ba as we have related, because his statement: 'Perfect the ablution' is the speech of Abu Hurayra, and his statement: 'Woe to the heels from the Fire' is the speech of the Prophet.' And most of Shu'ba's companions narrated this hadith and clarified that this addition is from the speech of Abu Hurayra. They are: 1. Adam ibn Abi Iyas, in al-Bukhari. 2. Hajjaj ibn Muhammad, in Ahmad. 3. Abu Dawud al-Tayalisi, as in his Musnad. 4. Asim ibn Ali, in al-Khatib. 5. Ali ibn al-Ja'd, in al-Khatib. 6. Isa ibn Yunus, in al-Khatib. 418. (continued list). And al-Bukhari narrated it as previously from the route of Adam ibn Abi Iyas, from Shu'ba, from Ibn Ziyad, from Abu Hurayra, who said: 'Perfect the ablution, for Abu al-Qasim (peace be upon him) said: 'Woe...'
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents a classic example of idraj (insertion) in hadith, where a narrator's own words are mistakenly included in the prophetic text. The hadith in question is about the importance of washing the feet completely during wudu. The phrase 'Awsighu al-wudu' (perfect the ablution) is actually the statement of Abu Hurayra, not the Prophet. The author lists fifteen narrators who correctly transmitted the hadith from Shu'ba without this insertion, thereby identifying the error made by Abu Qatan and Shababa. This demonstrates the meticulous work of hadith critics in distinguishing between the Prophet's words and those of narrators. The correct version, as recorded by al-Bukhari, includes Abu Hurayra saying 'Awsighu al-wudu' and then attributing only 'Waylun lil-a'qabi min al-nar' to the Prophet.

Key Terms
الإدراج (al-idraj): The insertion of a narrator's own words into the text of a hadith, often at the beginning or end, such that it appears part of the prophetic speech.
شاذ (shadhdh): An irregular narration where a trustworthy narrator contradicts a more reliable or numerous group. Here, the version with idraj is shadhdh.
موقوف (mawquf): A statement or action attributed to a Companion, not elevated to the Prophet. Here, 'Awsighu al-wudu' is mawquf from Abu Hurayra.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith about woe to the heels, which part is considered idraj (inserted)?
A) 'Waylun lil-a'qabi min al-nar'
B) 'Awsighu al-wudu'
C) Both parts
D) Neither part

Key Takeaway
Idraj must be identified through multiple chains; the correct prophetic text forms the basis for legal derivation in Hanafi usul.

Detecting Idraj at the Beginning: The Case of 'أسبغوا الوضوء'
Analysis of a documented case of idraj at the beginning of a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an example of idraj at the beginning of a hadith and evaluate the evidence used by hadith critics to distinguish between Prophetic and narrator speech.

Core Idea
A narrator's phrase mistaken for the Prophet's words can mislead fiqh derivation.

This passage from al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's work on idraj presents a documented case where two narrators erroneously included Abu Hurayrah's own instruction into the Prophetic hadith. Understanding how such interpolations are detected is crucial for usul al-fiqh, as it directly impacts the reliability of the textual source used for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the controversy
Locate the phrase 'أسبغوا الوضوء' in the hadith. Note that some narrators present it as part of the Prophet's speech, while others attribute it to Abu Hurayrah.
This is the central example of idraj at the beginning.
2
Count the narrators on each side
The author lists 15 narrators from Shu'bah who correctly report the phrase as Abu Hurayrah's statement, and only 2 (Abu Qatan and Shababah) who include it in the hadith.
The overwhelming majority of reliable narrators support the non-Prophetic origin.
3
Assess the rarity of the case
Ibn Hajar's investigation confirms that this example is extremely rare, contradicting al-Mahalli's claim that idraj at the beginning is common.
This highlights the meticulous nature of hadith criticism.
4
Analyze the consequence for istinbat
If the phrase were considered part of the hadith, it would appear that the Prophet commanded thorough wudu. As Abu Hurayrah's speech, it is a separate admonition, and the Prophetic text is only the warning about heels.
Correct identification of idraj is essential before deriving a ruling.
